    Concorde
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9rospatiale" title="Aérospatiale">
        Aérospatiale
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/British_Aircraft_Corporation" title="British Aircraft Corporation">
        BAC
       </a>
       <b>
        Concorde
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">
            ɒ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɔːr/: 'ar' in 'war'">
            ɔːr
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ) is a British-French
       <a href="/wiki/Turbojet" title="Turbojet">
        turbojet
       </a>
       -powered
       <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_aircraft" title="Supersonic aircraft">
        supersonic
       </a>
       passenger
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_airliner" title="Jet airliner">
        jet airliner
       </a>
       that was operated until 2003. It had a maximum speed over twice the
       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">
        speed of sound
       </a>
       at Mach 2.04 (1,354 mph or 2,180 km/h at cruise altitude), with seating for 92 to 128 passengers. First flown in 1969, Concorde entered service in 1976 and continued flying for the next 27 years. It is one of only two
       <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_transport" title="Supersonic transport">
        supersonic transports
       </a>
       to have been operated commercially; the other is the Soviet-built
       <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-144" title="Tupolev Tu-144">
        Tupolev Tu-144
       </a>
       , which operated in passenger service from 1977
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon_Tu-144_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gordon_Tu-144-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to 1978.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melik-Karamov_2000_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Melik-Karamov_2000-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde was jointly developed and manufactured by Sud Aviation (later Aérospatiale) and the British Aircraft Corporation (BAC) under an Anglo-French treaty. Twenty aircraft were built, including six prototypes and development aircraft.
       <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">
        Air France
       </a>
       (AF) and
       <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">
        British Airways
       </a>
       (BA) were the only airlines to purchase and fly Concorde. The aircraft was used mainly by wealthy passengers who could afford to pay a high price in exchange for Concorde's speed and luxury service. For example, in 1997, the round-trip ticket price from New York to London was $7995, more than 30 times the cost of the cheapest option to fly this route.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The original program cost estimate of £70 million
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       met huge overruns and delays, with the program eventually costing £1.3
       <i>
        billion
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was this extreme cost that became the main factor in the production run being much smaller than anticipated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another major factor which affected the viability of all
       <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_transport" title="Supersonic transport">
        supersonic transport
       </a>
       programmes was in how supersonic route options were eventually limited to ocean-crossing only to prevent
       <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">
        sonic boom
       </a>
       disturbance on populated areas. With only
       <a href="/wiki/Concorde_aircraft_histories#British_production_aircraft" title="Concorde aircraft histories">
        seven airframes
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Concorde_aircraft_histories#French_production_aircraft" title="Concorde aircraft histories">
        each
       </a>
       being operated by the British and French, the per-unit cost were impossible to recoup, so the French and British governments absorbed the development costs. British Airways and Air France were able to operate Concorde at a profit, in spite of very high maintenance costs, because Concorde was able to sustain a high ticket price.
      </p>
      <p>
       Among other
       <a href="#Scheduled_flights">
        destinations
       </a>
       , Concorde flew regular
       <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_flight" title="Transatlantic flight">
        transatlantic flights
       </a>
       from London's
       <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">
        Heathrow Airport
       </a>
       and Paris's
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle_Airport" title="Charles de Gaulle Airport">
        Charles de Gaulle Airport
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">
        John F. Kennedy International Airport
       </a>
       in New York,
       <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">
        Washington Dulles International Airport
       </a>
       in Virginia, and
       <a href="/wiki/Grantley_Adams_International_Airport" title="Grantley Adams International Airport">
        Grantley Adams International Airport
       </a>
       in Barbados; it flew these routes in less than half the time of other airliners.
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde's name, meaning "harmony" or "union", was chosen to reflect the co-operation on the project between the United Kingdom and France. In the UK, any or all of the type are known simply as
       <i>
        Concorde
       </i>
       , with no definite article
       <i>
        the
       </i>
       . Concorde won the 2006 Great British Design Quest organised by the BBC and the
       <a href="/wiki/Design_Museum" title="Design Museum">
        Design Museum
       </a>
       , beating other well-known designs such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BMC_Mini" title="BMC Mini">
        BMC Mini
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Miniskirt" title="Miniskirt">
        miniskirt
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_E-Type" title="Jaguar E-Type">
        Jaguar E-Type
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Tube_map" title="London Tube map">
        London Tube map
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire" title="Supermarine Spitfire">
        Supermarine Spitfire
       </a>
       . The type was retired in 2003, three years after the crash of
       <a href="/wiki/Air_France_Flight_4590" title="Air France Flight 4590">
        Air France Flight 4590
       </a>
       , in which all passengers and crew were killed. The general downturn in the commercial aviation industry after the
       <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
        September 11 attacks
       </a>
       in 2001 and the end of maintenance support for Concorde by
       <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">
        Airbus
       </a>
       (the successor company of both Aérospatiale and BAC) also contributed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Concorde_Grounded_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBC_Concorde_Grounded-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

           </a>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Development">
        Development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_studies">
        Early studies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early studies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The origins of the Concorde project date to the early 1950s, when
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arnold_Hall" title="Arnold Hall">
        Arnold Hall
       </a>
       , director of the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">
        Royal Aircraft Establishment
       </a>
       (RAE) asked
       <a href="/wiki/Morien_Morgan" title="Morien Morgan">
        Morien Morgan
       </a>
       to form a committee to study the supersonic transport (SST) concept. The group met for the first time in February 1954 and delivered their first report in April 1955.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200135_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200135-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At the time it was known that the drag at supersonic speeds was strongly related to the span of the wing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [N 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This led to the use of very short-span, very thin rectangular wings such as those seen on the control surfaces of many missiles, or in aircraft like the
       <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_F-104_Starfighter" title="Lockheed F-104 Starfighter">
        Lockheed F-104 Starfighter
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Avro_730" title="Avro 730">
        Avro 730
       </a>
       that the team studied. The team outlined a baseline configuration that looked like an enlarged Avro 730.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This same short span produced very little lift at low speed, which resulted in extremely long take-off runs and frighteningly high landing speeds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In an SST design, this would have required enormous engine power to lift off from existing runways, and to provide the fuel needed, "some horribly large aeroplanes" resulted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Based on this, the group considered the concept of an SST infeasible, and instead suggested continued low-level studies into supersonic aerodynamics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Slender_deltas">
        Slender deltas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Slender deltas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Soon after,
       <a href="/wiki/Johanna_Weber" title="Johanna Weber">
        Johanna Weber
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_K%C3%BCchemann" title="Dietrich Küchemann">
        Dietrich Küchemann
       </a>
       at the RAE published a series of reports on a new wing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planform_(aeronautics)" title="Planform (aeronautics)">
        planform
       </a>
       , known in the UK as the "slender delta" concept.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deltawing_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-deltawing-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aerosoc_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aerosoc-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The team, including Eric Maskell whose report "Flow Separation in Three Dimensions" contributed to an understanding of the physical nature of separated flow,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       worked with the fact that
       <a href="/wiki/Delta_wing" title="Delta wing">
        delta wings
       </a>
       can produce strong
       <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">
        vortexes
       </a>
       on their upper surfaces at high
       <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">
        angles of attack
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The vortex will lower the air pressure and cause lift to be greatly increased. This effect had been noticed earlier, notably by
       <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Yeager" title="Chuck Yeager">
        Chuck Yeager
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Convair_XF-92" title="Convair XF-92">
        Convair XF-92
       </a>
       , but its qualities had not been fully appreciated. Weber suggested that this was no mere curiosity, and the effect could be deliberately used to improve low speed performance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aerosoc_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-aerosoc-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Küchemann's and Weber's papers changed the entire nature of supersonic design almost overnight. Although the delta had already been used on aircraft prior to this point, these designs used planforms that were not much different from a
       <a href="/wiki/Swept_wing" title="Swept wing">
        swept wing
       </a>
       of the same span.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [N 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Weber noted that the lift from the vortex was increased by the length of the wing it had to operate over, which suggested that the effect would be maximised by extending the wing along the fuselage as far as possible. Such a layout would still have good supersonic performance inherent to the short span, while also offering reasonable take-off and landing speeds using vortex generation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aerosoc_18-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-aerosoc-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The only downside to such a design is that the aircraft would have to take off and land very "nose high" to generate the required vortex lift, which led to questions about the low speed handling qualities of such a design.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-brown-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It would also need to have long
       <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">
        landing gear
       </a>
       to produce the required angle of attack while still on the runway.
      </p>
      <p>
       Küchemann presented the idea at a meeting where Morgan was also present. Test pilot
       <a href="/wiki/Eric_Brown_(pilot)" title="Eric Brown (pilot)">
        Eric Brown
       </a>
       recalls Morgan's reaction to the presentation, saying that he immediately seized on it as the solution to the SST problem. Brown considers this moment as being the true birth of the Concorde project.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-brown-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Supersonic_Transport_Advisory_Committee">
        Supersonic Transport Advisory Committee
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Supersonic Transport Advisory Committee">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HP.115.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="381" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/HP.115.gif/220px-HP.115.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/HP.115.gif/330px-HP.115.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/HP.115.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HP.115.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The HP.115 tested the low-speed performance of the slender delta layout.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 1 October 1956 the
       <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Supply" title="Ministry of Supply">
        Ministry of Supply
       </a>
       asked Morgan to form a new study group, the
       <i>
        Supersonic Transport Advisory Committee
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        STAC
       </i>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200539_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200539-22">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with the explicit goal of developing a practical SST design and finding industry partners to build it. At the very first meeting, on 5 November 1956, the decision was made to fund the development of a test bed aircraft to examine the low-speed performance of the slender delta, a contract that eventually produced the
       <a href="/wiki/Handley_Page_HP.115" title="Handley Page HP.115">
        Handley Page HP.115
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_21-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-brown-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This aircraft would ultimately demonstrate safe control at speeds as low as 69 mph, about ⅓ that of the F-104 Starfighter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinchester2005b134_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinchester2005b134-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       STAC stated that an SST would have economic performance similar to existing subsonic types.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although they would burn more fuel in cruise, they would be able to fly more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sorties" title="Sorties">
        sorties
       </a>
       in a given period of time, so fewer aircraft would be needed to service a particular route. This would remain economically advantageous as long as fuel represented a small percentage of operational costs, as it did at the time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       STAC suggested that two designs naturally fell out of their work, a transatlantic model flying at about Mach 2, and a shorter-range version flying at perhaps Mach 1.2. Morgan suggested that a 150-passenger transatlantic SST would cost about £75 to £90 million to develop, and be in service in 1970. The smaller 100 passenger short-range version would cost perhaps £50 to £80 million, and be ready for service in 1968. To meet this schedule, development would need to begin in 1960, with production contracts let in 1962.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200567_15-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200567-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Morgan strongly suggested that the US was already involved in a similar project, and that if the UK failed to respond it would be locked out of an airliner market that he believed would be dominated by SST aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200568_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200568-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1959, a study contract was awarded to
       <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley" title="Hawker Siddeley">
        Hawker Siddeley
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Aeroplane_Company" title="Bristol Aeroplane Company">
        Bristol
       </a>
       for preliminary designs based on the slender delta concept,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200569_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200569-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which developed as the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hawker_Siddeley_HSA.1000&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hawker Siddeley HSA.1000 (page does not exist)">
        HSA.1000
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bristol_223" title="Bristol 223">
        Bristol 198
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Armstrong_Whitworth" title="Armstrong Whitworth">
        Armstrong Whitworth
       </a>
       also responded with an internal design, the M-Wing, for the lower-speed shorter-range category. Even at this early time, both the STAC group and the government were looking for partners to develop the designs. In September 1959, Hawker approached
       <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Corporation" title="Lockheed Corporation">
        Lockheed
       </a>
       , and after the creation of
       <a href="/wiki/British_Aircraft_Corporation" title="British Aircraft Corporation">
        British Aircraft Corporation
       </a>
       in 1960, the former Bristol team immediately started talks with
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">
        Boeing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics" title="General Dynamics">
        General Dynamics
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Douglas_Aircraft" title="Douglas Aircraft">
        Douglas Aircraft
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sud_Aviation" title="Sud Aviation">
        Sud Aviation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200569_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200569-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ogee_planform_selected">
        Ogee planform selected
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ogee planform selected">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Küchemann and others at the RAE continued their work on the slender delta throughout this period, considering three basic shapes; the classic straight-edge delta, the "gothic delta" that was rounded outwards to appear like a
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture#Equilateral_arch" title="Gothic architecture">
        gothic arch
       </a>
       , and the "
       <a href="/wiki/Ogive" title="Ogive">
        ogival
       </a>
       wing" that was compound-rounded into the shape of an
       <a href="/wiki/Ogee" title="Ogee">
        ogee
       </a>
       . Each of these planforms had its own advantages and disadvantages in terms of aerodynamics. As they worked with these shapes, a practical concern grew to become so important that it forced selection of one of these designs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200140_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200140-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Generally one wants to have the wing's
       <a href="/wiki/Center_of_pressure_(fluid_mechanics)" title="Center of pressure (fluid mechanics)">
        centre of pressure
       </a>
       (CP, or "lift point") close to the aircraft's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_of_gravity" title="Centre of gravity">
        centre of gravity
       </a>
       (CG, or "balance point") to reduce the amount of control force required to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch_axis_(kinematics)" title="Pitch axis (kinematics)">
        pitch
       </a>
       the aircraft. As the aircraft layout changes during the design phase, it is common for the CG to move fore or aft. With a normal wing design this can be addressed by moving the wing slightly fore or aft to account for this.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [N 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With a delta wing running most of the length of the fuselage, this was no longer easy; moving the wing would leave it in front of the nose or behind the tail. Studying the various layouts in terms of CG changes, both during design and changes due to fuel use during flight, the ogee planform immediately came to the fore.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200140_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200140-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="199" data-file-width="412" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png/200px-Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png/300px-Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png/400px-Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bristol_Type_223_top-view_silhouette.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Plan-view silhouette of the Bristol Type 223 SST project
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       While the wing planform was evolving, so was the basic SST concept. Bristol's original Type 198 was a small design with an almost pure slender delta wing,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but evolved into the larger
       <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Type_223" title="Bristol Type 223">
        Type 223
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Partnership_with_Sud_Aviation">
        Partnership with Sud Aviation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Partnership with Sud Aviation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       By this time similar political and economic concerns in France had led to their own SST plans. In the late 1950s the government requested designs from both the government-owned Sud Aviation and
       <a href="/wiki/Nord_Aviation" title="Nord Aviation">
        Nord Aviation
       </a>
       , as well as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dassault" title="Dassault">
        Dassault
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [N 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All three returned designs based on Küchemann and Weber's slender delta; Nord suggested a
       <a href="/wiki/Ramjet" title="Ramjet">
        ramjet
       </a>
       powered design flying at Mach 3, the other two were jet powered Mach 2 designs that were similar to each other. Of the three, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sud_Aviation_Super-Caravelle" title="Sud Aviation Super-Caravelle">
        Sud Aviation Super-Caravelle
       </a>
       won the design contest with a medium-range design deliberately sized to avoid competition with transatlantic US designs they assumed were already on the drawing board.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200570_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200570-30">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As soon as the design was complete, in April 1960, Pierre Satre, the company's technical director, was sent to Bristol to discuss a partnership. Bristol was surprised to find that the Sud team had designed a very similar aircraft after considering the SST problem and coming to the very same conclusions as the Bristol and STAC teams in terms of economics. It was later revealed that the original STAC report, marked "For UK Eyes Only", had secretly been passed to the French to win political favour. Sud made minor changes to the paper, and presented it as their own work.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200149_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200149-31">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Unsurprisingly, the two teams found much to agree on. The French had no modern large jet engines, and had already concluded they would buy a British design anyway (as they had on the earlier subsonic
       <a href="/wiki/Sud_Aviation_Caravelle" title="Sud Aviation Caravelle">
        Caravelle
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200147_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200147-32">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As neither company had experience in the use of high-heat metals for airframes, a maximum speed of around Mach 2 was selected so aluminium could be used – above this speed the friction with the air warms the metal so much that aluminium begins to soften. This lower speed would also speed development and allow their design to fly before the Americans. Finally, everyone involved agreed that Küchemann's ogee shaped wing was the right one.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200570_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200570-30">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The only disagreements were over the size and range. The UK team was still focused on a 150-passenger design serving transatlantic routes, while the French were deliberately avoiding these. However, this proved not to be the barrier it might seem; common components could be used in both designs, with the shorter range version using a clipped fuselage and four engines, the longer one with a stretched fuselage and six engines, leaving only the wing to be extensively re-designed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200141_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200141-33">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The teams continued to meet through 1961, and by this time it was clear that the two aircraft would be considerably more similar in spite of different range and seating arrangements. A single design emerged that differed mainly in fuel load. More powerful
       <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Olympus" title="Rolls-Royce Olympus">
        Bristol Siddeley Olympus
       </a>
       engines, being developed for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TSR-2" title="TSR-2">
        TSR-2
       </a>
       , allowed either design to be powered by only four engines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200150_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200150-34">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Cabinet_response.2C_treaty">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet_response,_treaty">
        Cabinet response, treaty
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cabinet response, treaty">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       While the development teams met, French Minister of Public Works and Transport
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Buron" title="Robert Buron">
        Robert Buron
       </a>
       was meeting with the UK Minister of Aviation
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thorneycroft" title="Peter Thorneycroft">
        Peter Thorneycroft
       </a>
       , and Thorneycroft soon revealed to the cabinet that the French were much more serious about a partnership than any of the US companies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200571_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200571-35">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The various US companies had proved uninterested in such a venture, likely due to the belief that the government would be funding development and would frown on any partnership with a European company, and the risk of "giving away" US technological leadership to a European partner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200569_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200569-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When the STAC plans were presented to the UK cabinet, a very negative reaction resulted. The economic considerations were considered highly questionable, especially as these were based on development costs, now estimated to be £150 million, which were repeatedly overrun in the industry. The Treasury Ministry in particular presented a very negative view, suggesting that there was no way the project would have any positive financial returns for the government, especially in light that "the industry's past record of over-optimistic estimating (including the recent history of the TSR.2) suggests that it would be prudent to consider the £150 million [cost] to turn out much too low."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200571_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200571-35">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This concern led to an independent review of the project by the Committee on Civil Scientific Research and Development, which met on topic between July and September 1962. The Committee ultimately rejected the economic arguments, including considerations of supporting the industry made by Thorneycroft. Their report in October stated that it was unlikely there would be any direct positive economic outcome, but that the project should still be considered for the simple reason that everyone else was going supersonic, and they were concerned they would be locked out of future markets. Conversely, it appeared the project would not be likely to significantly impact other, more important, research efforts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200571_35-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200571-35">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After considerable argument, the decision to proceed ultimately fell to an unlikely political expediency. At the time, the UK was pressing for admission to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Common_Market" title="European Common Market">
        European Common Market
       </a>
       , which was being controlled by
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">
        Charles de Gaulle
       </a>
       who felt the UK's
       <a href="/wiki/Special_Relationship" title="Special Relationship">
        Special Relationship
       </a>
       with the US made them unacceptable in a pan-European group. Cabinet felt that signing a deal with Sud would pave the way for Common Market entry, and this became the main deciding reason for moving ahead with the deal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200566_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200566-36">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was this belief that had led the original STAC documents being leaked to the French. However, De Gaulle spoke of the European origin of the design, and continued to block the UK's entry into the Common Market.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConway200566_36-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConway200566-36">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The development project was negotiated as an international treaty between the two countries rather than a commercial agreement between companies and included a clause, originally asked for by the UK, imposing heavy penalties for cancellation. A draft treaty was signed on 29 November 1962.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earlyhist_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-earlyhist-37">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">
        Naming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Naming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Reflecting the treaty between the British and French governments that led to Concorde's construction, the name
       <i>
        Concorde
       </i>
       is from the French word
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/concorde#French" title="wikt:concorde">
         concorde
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <small>
        IPA:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">
         [kɔ̃kɔʁd]
        </a>
       </span>
       ), which has an English equivalent,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/concord#English" title="wikt:concord">
         concord
        </a>
       </i>
       . Both words mean
       <i>
        agreement
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        harmony
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        union
       </i>
       . The name was officially changed to
       <i>
        Concord
       </i>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">
        Harold Macmillan
       </a>
       in response to a perceived slight by
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">
        Charles de Gaulle
       </a>
       . At the French roll-out in
       <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">
        Toulouse
       </a>
       in late 1967,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssccunv_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ssccunv-38">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the British Government
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_for_Technology" title="Minister for Technology">
        Minister for Technology
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">
        Tony Benn
       </a>
       , announced that he would change the spelling back to
       <i>
        Concorde
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benn_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Benn-39">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This created a nationalist uproar that died down when Benn stated that the suffixed "e" represented "Excellence, England, Europe and
       <a href="/wiki/Entente_Cordiale" title="Entente Cordiale">
        Entente (Cordiale)
       </a>
       ". In his memoirs, he recounts a tale of a letter from an irate Scotsman claiming: "[Y]ou talk about 'E' for England, but part of it is made in Scotland." Given Scotland's contribution of providing the nose cone for the aircraft, Benn replied, "[I]t was also 'E' for 'Écosse' (the French name for Scotland) – and I might have added 'e' for extravagance and 'e' for escalation as well!"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcIntyre199220_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcIntyre199220-40">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde also acquired an unusual
       <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">
        nomenclature
       </a>
       for an aircraft. In common usage in the United Kingdom, the type is known as
       <i>
        Concorde
       </i>
       without an
       <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">
        article
       </a>
       , rather than
       <i>
        <b>
         the
        </b>
        Concorde
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        Concorde
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_efforts">
        Sales efforts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sales efforts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Concorde.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="709" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/British_Concorde.jpg/250px-British_Concorde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/British_Concorde.jpg/375px-British_Concorde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/British_Concorde.jpg/500px-British_Concorde.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Concorde.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">
          British Airways
         </a>
         Concorde in early BA livery at
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London-Heathrow_Airport" title="London-Heathrow Airport">
          London-Heathrow Airport
         </a>
         , in the early 1980s
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Described by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Flight_International" title="Flight International">
         Flight International
        </a>
       </i>
       as an "aviation icon" and "one of aerospace's most ambitious but commercially flawed projects",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde failed to meet its original sales targets, despite initial interest from several airlines.
      </p>
      <p>
       At first, the new consortium intended to produce one long-range and one short-range version. However, prospective customers showed no interest in the short-range version and it was dropped.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earlyhist_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-earlyhist-37">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An advertisement covering two full pages, promoting Concorde, ran in the 29 May 1967 issue of
       <i>
        Aviation Week &amp; Space Technology
       </i>
       . The advertisement predicted a market for 350 aircraft by 1980 and boasted of Concorde's head start over the United States' SST project.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde had considerable difficulties that led to its dismal sales performance. Costs had spiralled during development to more than six times the original projections, arriving at a unit cost of £23 million in 1977 (equivalent to £131.14 million in 2016).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its sonic boom made travelling supersonically over land impossible without causing complaints from citizens.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concordes_limited_to_16_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Concordes_limited_to_16-47">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       World events had also dampened Concorde sales prospects, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1973-74_stock_market_crash" title="1973-74 stock market crash">
        1973-74 stock market crash
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">
        1973 oil crisis
       </a>
       had made many airlines cautious about aircraft with high fuel consumption rates; and new
       <a href="/wiki/Wide-body_aircraft" title="Wide-body aircraft">
        wide-body aircraft
       </a>
       , such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">
        Boeing 747
       </a>
       , had recently made subsonic aircraft significantly more efficient and presented a low-risk option for airlines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss197847–49_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss197847–49-48">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While carrying a full load, Concorde achieved 15.8
       <a href="/wiki/Passenger_miles_per_gallon" title="Passenger miles per gallon">
        passenger miles per gallon
       </a>
       of fuel, while the
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_707" title="Boeing 707">
        Boeing 707
       </a>
       reached 33.3 pm/g, the Boeing 747 46.4 pm/g, and the
       <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-10" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-10">
        McDonnell Douglas DC-10
       </a>
       53.6 pm/g.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss197849_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss197849-49">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An emerging trend in the industry in favour of cheaper airline tickets had also caused airlines such as
       <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">
        Qantas
       </a>
       to question Concorde's market suitability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The consortium secured orders (i.e., non-binding options) for over 100 of the long-range version from the major airlines of the day:
       <a href="/wiki/Pan_American_World_Airways" title="Pan American World Airways">
        Pan Am
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">
        BOAC
       </a>
       , and Air France were the launch customers, with six Concordes each. Other airlines in the order book included
       <a href="/wiki/Panair_do_Brasil" title="Panair do Brasil">
        Panair do Brasil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Continental_Airlines" title="Continental Airlines">
        Continental Airlines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Japan_Airlines" title="Japan Airlines">
        Japan Airlines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">
        Lufthansa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">
        American Airlines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">
        United Airlines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Air_India" title="Air India">
        Air India
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Air_Canada" title="Air Canada">
        Air Canada
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Braniff_International_Airways" title="Braniff International Airways">
        Braniff
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">
        Singapore Airlines
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Iran_Air" title="Iran Air">
        Iran Air
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_Airways" title="Olympic Airways">
        Olympic Airways
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">
        Qantas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Administration_of_China" title="Civil Aviation Administration of China">
        CAAC
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East_Airlines" title="Middle East Airlines">
        Middle East Airlines
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Trans_World_Airlines" title="Trans World Airlines">
        TWA
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earlyhist_37-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-earlyhist-37">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the time of the first flight the options list contained 74 options from 16 airlines:
      </p>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>

          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">

           </a>
