      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">
        Testing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Testing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The design work was supported by a preceding research programme studying the flight characteristics of low ratio
       <a href="/wiki/Delta_wing" title="Delta wing">
        delta wings
       </a>
       . A supersonic
       <a href="/wiki/Fairey_Delta_2" title="Fairey Delta 2">
        Fairey Delta 2
       </a>
       was modified to carry the ogee planform, and, renamed as the BAC 221, used for flight tests of the high speed flight envelope,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor1965130_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor1965130-54">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Handley_Page_HP.115" title="Handley Page HP.115">
        Handley Page HP.115
       </a>
       also provided valuable information on low speed performance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinchester2005a134_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinchester2005a134-55">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_(1969)_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1351" data-file-width="1966" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_%281969%29_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg/220px-02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_%281969%29_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_%281969%29_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg/330px-02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_%281969%29_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_%281969%29_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg/440px-02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_%281969%29_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:02.03.69_1er_vol_de_Concorde_(1969)_-_53Fi1931_-_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concorde 001 first flight in 1969
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Construction of two prototypes began in February 1965: 001, built by Aérospatiale at Toulouse, and 002, by BAC at
       <a href="/wiki/Filton" title="Filton">
        Filton
       </a>
       , Bristol. Concorde 001 made its first test flight from Toulouse on 2 March 1969, piloted by
       <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Turcat" title="André Turcat">
        André Turcat
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and first went supersonic on 1 October.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first UK-built Concorde flew from Filton to
       <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fairford" title="RAF Fairford">
        RAF Fairford
       </a>
       on 9 April 1969, piloted by
       <a href="/wiki/Brian_Trubshaw" title="Brian Trubshaw">
        Brian Trubshaw
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both prototypes were presented to the public for the first time on 7–8 June 1969 at the
       <a href="/wiki/Paris_Air_Show" title="Paris Air Show">
        Paris Air Show
       </a>
       . As the flight programme progressed, 001 embarked on a sales and demonstration tour on 4 September 1971, which was also the first transatlantic crossing of Concorde.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde 002 followed suit on 2 June 1972 with a tour of the Middle and Far East.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde 002 made the first visit to the United States in 1973, landing at the new
       <a href="/wiki/Dallas/Fort_Worth_International_Airport" title="Dallas/Fort Worth International Airport">
        Dallas/Fort Worth Regional Airport
       </a>
       to mark that airport's opening.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="1023" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg/220px-Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg/330px-Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg/440px-Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_first_visit_Heathrow_Fitzgerald.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concorde on early visit to Heathrow Airport on 1 July 1972
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       While Concorde had initially held a great deal of customer interest, the project was hit by a large number of order cancellations. The
       <a href="/wiki/1973_Paris_Air_Show_crash" title="1973 Paris Air Show crash">
        Paris Le Bourget air show crash
       </a>
       of the competing Soviet
       <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-144" title="Tupolev Tu-144">
        Tupolev Tu-144
       </a>
       had shocked potential buyers, and public concern over the environmental issues presented by a supersonic aircraft – the
       <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">
        sonic boom
       </a>
       , take-off noise and pollution – had produced a shift in public opinion of SSTs. By 1976 four nations remained as prospective buyers: Britain, France, China, and Iran.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concordes_limited_to_16_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Concordes_limited_to_16-47">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Only Air France and British Airways (the successor to BOAC) took up their orders, with the two governments taking a cut of any profits made.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The United States cancelled the
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_2707" title="Boeing 2707">
        Boeing 2707
       </a>
       , its rival supersonic transport programme, in 1971. Observers have suggested that opposition to Concorde on grounds of noise pollution had been encouraged by the United States Government, as it lacked its own competitor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The US, India, and Malaysia all ruled out Concorde supersonic flights over the noise concern, although some of these restrictions were later relaxed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Professor Douglas Ross characterised restrictions placed upon Concorde operations by President
       <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">
        Jimmy Carter
       </a>
       's administration as having been an act of
       <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">
        protectionism
       </a>
       of American aircraft manufacturers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoss197846_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoss197846-68">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde flew to an altitude of 68,000 ft (20,700 m) during a test flight in June 1973.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Design">
        Design
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Design">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg/220px-ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg/330px-ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg/440px-ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ConcordeCockpitSinsheim.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concorde flight deck layout
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="General_features">
        General features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: General features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Concorde is an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ogival_delta" title="Ogival delta">
        ogival delta
       </a>
       winged aircraft with four
       <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce/Snecma_Olympus_593" title="Rolls-Royce/Snecma Olympus 593">
        Olympus
       </a>
       engines based on those employed in the RAF's
       <a href="/wiki/Avro_Vulcan" title="Avro Vulcan">
        Avro Vulcan
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bomber" title="Strategic bomber">
        strategic bomber
       </a>
       . It is one of the few commercial aircraft to employ a
       <a href="/wiki/Tailless_aircraft" title="Tailless aircraft">
        tailless design
       </a>
       (the
       <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-144" title="Tupolev Tu-144">
        Tupolev Tu-144
       </a>
       being another). Concorde was the first airliner to have a (in this case, analogue)
       <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">
        fly-by-wire
       </a>
       flight-control system; the
       <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">
        avionics
       </a>
       system Concorde used was unique because it was the first commercial aircraft to employ
       <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_integrated_circuit" title="Hybrid integrated circuit">
        hybrid circuits
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elecflybywire_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-elecflybywire-70">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The principal designer for the project was Pierre Satre, with
       <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Russell" title="Archibald Russell">
        Sir Archibald Russell
       </a>
       as his deputy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde pioneered the following technologies:
      </p>
      <p>
       For high speed and optimisation of flight:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_delta" title="Double delta">
         Double delta
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Ogee" title="Ogee">
         ogee
        </a>
        /ogival) shaped wings
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deltawing_17-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-deltawing-17">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Variable engine air
        <a href="/wiki/Intake_ramp" title="Intake ramp">
         intake ramp
        </a>
        system controlled by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_computer" title="Digital computer">
         digital computers
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nova_72-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nova-72">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Supercruise" title="Supercruise">
         Supercruise
        </a>
        capability
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
         <a href="#cite_note-73">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Thrust-by-wire engines, predecessor of today's
        <a href="/wiki/FADEC" title="FADEC">
         FADEC
        </a>
        -controlled engines
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nova_72-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-nova-72">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Droop-nose" title="Droop-nose">
         Droop-nose
        </a>
        section for better landing visibility
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       For weight-saving and enhanced performance:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">
         Mach
        </a>
        2.04 (~2,179 km/h or 1,354 mph) cruising speed
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrawley200314_74-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrawley200314-74">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
        for optimum fuel consumption (supersonic drag minimum and turbojet engines are more efficient at higher speed
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
         <a href="#cite_note-75">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ) Fuel consumption at Mach 2.0 and at altitude of 60,000 feet (18,000 m) was 4,800 gallons per hour (22,000 l/h).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
         <a href="#cite_note-76">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Mainly aluminium construction using a high temperature alloy similar to that developed for aero-engine pistons.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
         <a href="#cite_note-77">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This material gave low weight and allowed conventional manufacture (higher speeds would have ruled out aluminium)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
         <a href="#cite_note-78">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Full-regime
        <a href="/wiki/Autopilot" title="Autopilot">
         autopilot
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Autothrottle" title="Autothrottle">
         autothrottle
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
         <a href="#cite_note-79">
          [75]
         </a>
        </sup>
        allowing "hands off" control of the aircraft from climb out to landing
       </li>
       <li>
        Fully electrically controlled analogue
        <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_flight_control_system#Fly-by-wire_control_systems" title="Aircraft flight control system">
         fly-by-wire
        </a>
        flight controls systems
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elecflybywire_70-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-elecflybywire-70">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        High-pressure hydraulic system using 28 MPa (4,000 lbf/in²) for lighter hydraulic components,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
         <a href="#cite_note-80">
          [76]
         </a>
        </sup>
        tripled independent systems ("Blue", "Green", and "Yellow") for redundancy, with an emergency
        <a href="/wiki/Ram_air_turbine" title="Ram air turbine">
         ram air turbine
        </a>
        (RAT) stored in the port-inner elevon jack fairing supplying "Green" and "Yellow" as backup.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
         <a href="#cite_note-81">
          [77]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Complex
        <a href="/wiki/Air_data_computer" title="Air data computer">
         Air data computer
        </a>
        (ADC) for the automated monitoring and transmission of aerodynamic measurements (total pressure,
        <a href="/wiki/Static_pressure" title="Static pressure">
         static pressure
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">
         angle of attack
        </a>
        , side-slip).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen2001101_82-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen2001101-82">
          [78]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Fully electrically controlled analogue
        <a href="/wiki/Brake-by-wire" title="Brake-by-wire">
         brake-by-wire
        </a>
        system
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
         <a href="#cite_note-83">
          [79]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Pitch trim by shifting fuel fore-and-aft for
        <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#Center_of_gravity" title="Center of mass">
         centre-of-gravity
        </a>
        (CofG) control at the approach to Mach 1 and above with no drag penalty.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
         <a href="#cite_note-84">
          [80]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Pitch trimming by fuel transfer had been used since 1958 on the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-58" title="B-58">
         B-58
        </a>
        supersonic bomber.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
         <a href="#cite_note-85">
          [81]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Parts made using "
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milling_machine" title="Milling machine">
         sculpture milling
        </a>
        ", reducing the part count while saving weight and adding strength.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
         <a href="#cite_note-86">
          [82]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        No
        <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_power_unit" title="Auxiliary power unit">
         auxiliary power unit
        </a>
        , as Concorde would only visit large airports where
        <a href="/wiki/Ground_support_equipment#Air_Start_Unit_(ASU)" title="Ground support equipment">
         ground air start carts
        </a>
        are available.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen2001206_87-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen2001206-87">
          [83]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Powerplant">
        Powerplant
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Powerplant">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outlet_Concorde.JPG">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Outlet_Concorde.JPG/220px-Outlet_Concorde.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Outlet_Concorde.JPG/330px-Outlet_Concorde.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Outlet_Concorde.JPG/440px-Outlet_Concorde.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Close up of engine nozzles of production Concorde G-AXDN. The nozzle consists of tilting cups.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concordeintake.gif">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="938" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Concordeintake.gif/220px-Concordeintake.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Concordeintake.gif/330px-Concordeintake.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Concordeintake.gif/440px-Concordeintake.gif 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Concorde's
          <a href="/wiki/Intake_ramp" title="Intake ramp">
           intake ramp
          </a>
          system schematics
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_Ramp.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Concorde_Ramp.jpg/220px-Concorde_Ramp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Concorde_Ramp.jpg/330px-Concorde_Ramp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Concorde_Ramp.jpg/440px-Concorde_Ramp.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Concorde's intake ramp system
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce/Snecma_Olympus_593" title="Rolls-Royce/Snecma Olympus 593">
        Rolls-Royce/Snecma Olympus 593
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A symposium titled "Supersonic-Transport Implications" was hosted by the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aeronautical_Society" title="Royal Aeronautical Society">
        Royal Aeronautical Society
       </a>
       on 8 December 1960. Various views were put forward on the likely type of powerplant for a supersonic transport, such as podded or buried installation and turbojet or ducted-fan engines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Boundary layer management in the podded installation was put forward as simpler with only an inlet cone but Dr. Seddon of the RAE saw "a future in a more sophisticated integration of shapes" in a buried installation. Another concern highlighted the case with two or more engines situated behind a single intake. An intake failure could lead to a double or triple engine failure. The advantage of the ducted fan over the turbojet was reduced airport noise but with considerable economic penalties with its larger cross-section producing excessive drag.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At that time it was considered that the noise from a turbojet optimised for supersonic cruise could be reduced to an acceptable level using noise suppressors as used on subsonic jets.
      </p>
      <p>
       The powerplant configuration selected for Concorde, and its development to a certificated design, can be seen in light of the above symposium topics (which highlighted airfield noise, boundary layer management and interactions between adjacent engines) and the requirement that the powerplant, at Mach 2, tolerate combinations of pushovers, sideslips, pull-ups and throttle slamming without surging.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-talbot-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         p.131
        </span>
       </sup>
       Extensive development testing with design changes and changes to intake and engine control laws would address most of the issues except airfield noise and the interaction between adjacent powerplants at speeds above Mach 1.6 which meant Concorde "had to be certified aerodynamically as a twin-engined aircraft above Mach 1.6".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_91-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-talbot-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Rolls-Royce had a design proposal, the RB.169, for the aircraft at the time of Concorde's initial design
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but "to develop a brand-new engine for Concorde would have been prohibitively expensive"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       so an existing engine, already flying in the
       <a href="/wiki/BAC_TSR-2" title="BAC TSR-2">
        TSR-2
       </a>
       prototype, was chosen. It was the Olympus 320 turbojet, a development of the Bristol engine first used for the
       <a href="/wiki/Avro_Vulcan" title="Avro Vulcan">
        Avro Vulcan
       </a>
       bomber.
      </p>
      <p>
       Great confidence was placed in being able to reduce the noise of a turbojet and massive strides by SNECMA in silencer design were reported during the programme.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, by 1974 the spade silencers which projected into the exhaust were reported to be ineffective.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Olympus Mk.622 with reduced jet velocity was proposed to reduce the noise
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but it was not developed.
      </p>
      <p>
       Situated behind the leading edge of the wing the engine intake had wing boundary layer ahead of it. Two thirds was diverted and the remaining third which entered the intake did not adversely affect the intake efficiency
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_91-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-talbot-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         p.21
        </span>
       </sup>
       except during pushovers when the boundary layer thickened ahead of the intake and caused surging. Extensive wind tunnel testing helped define leading edge modifications ahead of the intakes which solved the problem.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Each engine had its own intake and the engine nacelles were paired with a splitter plate between them to minimise adverse behaviour of one powerplant influencing the other. Only above Mach 1.6 was an engine surge likely to affect the adjacent engine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot_91-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-talbot-91">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde needed to fly long distances to be economically viable; this required high efficiency from the powerplant.
       <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">
        Turbofan
       </a>
       engines were rejected due to their larger cross-section producing excessive drag. Olympus
       <a href="/wiki/Turbojet" title="Turbojet">
        turbojet
       </a>
       technology was available to be developed to meet the design requirements of the aircraft, although
       <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">
        turbofans
       </a>
       would be studied for any future SST.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The aircraft used reheat (
       <a href="/wiki/Afterburner" title="Afterburner">
        afterburners
       </a>
       ) at take-off and to pass through the upper
       <a href="/wiki/Transonic" title="Transonic">
        transonic
       </a>
       regime and to supersonic speeds, between Mach 0.95 and Mach 1.7. The afterburners were switched off at all other times.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to jet engines being
       <a href="/wiki/Propulsive_efficiency" title="Propulsive efficiency">
        highly inefficient at low speeds
       </a>
       , Concorde burned two tonnes of fuel (almost 2% of the maximum fuel load) taxiing to the runway.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fuel used is
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">
        Jet A-1
       </a>
       . Due to the high thrust produced even with the engines at idle, only the two outer engines were run after landing for easier taxiing and less
       <a href="/wiki/Brake_pad" title="Brake pad">
        brake pad
       </a>
       wear - at low weights after landing, the aircraft would not remain stationary with all four engines idling requiring the brakes to be continuously applied to prevent the aircraft from rolling.
      </p>
      <p>
       The intake design for Concorde's engines was especially critical.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The intakes had to provide low distortion levels (to prevent engine surge) and high efficiency for all likely ambient temperatures to be met in cruise. They had to provide adequate subsonic performance for diversion cruise and low engine-face distortion at take-off. They also had to provide an alternate path for excess intake air during engine throttling or shutdowns.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The variable intake features required to meet all these requirements consisted of front and rear ramps, a dump door, an auxiliary inlet and a ramp bleed to the exhaust nozzle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As well as supplying air to the engine, the intake also supplied air through the ramp bleed to the propelling nozzle. The nozzle ejector (or aerodynamic) design, with variable exit area and secondary flow from the intake, contributed to good expansion efficiency from take-off to cruise.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Engine failure causes problems on conventional
       <a href="/wiki/Subsonic_aircraft" title="Subsonic aircraft">
        subsonic aircraft
       </a>
       ; not only does the aircraft lose thrust on that side but the engine creates drag, causing the aircraft to yaw and bank in the direction of the failed engine. If this had happened to Concorde at supersonic speeds, it theoretically could have caused a catastrophic failure of the airframe. Although computer simulations predicted considerable problems, in practice Concorde could shut down both engines on the same side of the aircraft at Mach 2 without the predicted difficulties.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During an engine failure the required air intake is virtually zero. So, on Concorde, engine failure was countered by the opening of the auxiliary spill door and the full extension of the ramps, which deflected the air downwards past the engine, gaining lift and minimising drag. Concorde pilots were routinely trained to handle double engine failure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde's Air Intake Control Units (AICUs) made use of a digital processor to provide the necessary accuracy for intake control. It was the world's first use of a digital processor to be given full authority control of an essential system in a passenger aircraft. It was developed by the Electronics and Space Systems (ESS) division of the British Aircraft Corporation after it became clear that the analogue AICUs fitted to the prototype aircraft and developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Ultra_Electronics" title="Ultra Electronics">
        Ultra Electronics
       </a>
       were found to be insufficiently accurate for the tasks in hand.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde's thrust-by-wire engine control system was developed by Ultra Electronics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Heating_problems">
        Heating problems
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Heating problems">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Air compression on the outer surfaces caused the cabin to heat up during flight. Every surface, such as windows and panels, was warm to the touch by the end of the flight.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Besides engines, the hottest part of the structure of any supersonic aircraft is the
       <a href="/wiki/Nose_cone" title="Nose cone">
        nose
       </a>
       , due to
       <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_heating" title="Aerodynamic heating">
        aerodynamic heating
       </a>
       . The engineers used
       <a href="/wiki/Hiduminium" title="Hiduminium">
        Hiduminium R.R. 58
       </a>
       , an aluminium alloy, throughout the aircraft because of its familiarity, cost and ease of construction. The highest temperature that aluminium could sustain over the life of the aircraft was 127 °C (261 °F), which limited the top speed to Mach 2.02.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde went through two cycles of heating and cooling during a flight, first cooling down as it gained altitude, then heating up after going supersonic. The reverse happened when descending and slowing down. This had to be factored into the metallurgical and
       <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(material)" title="Fatigue (material)">
        fatigue
       </a>
       modelling. A test rig was built that repeatedly heated up a full-size section of the wing, and then cooled it, and periodically samples of metal were taken for testing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Concorde airframe was designed for a life of 45,000 flying hours.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="634" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg/220px-Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg/330px-Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg/440px-Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_-_airframe_temperatures.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concorde skin temperatures
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Owing to
       <a href="/wiki/Ram_pressure" title="Ram pressure">
        air compression
       </a>
       in front of the plane as it travelled at
       <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_speed" title="Supersonic speed">
        supersonic speed
       </a>
       , the fuselage heated up and
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">
        expanded
       </a>
       by as much as 300 mm (almost 1 ft). The most obvious manifestation of this was a gap that opened up on the flight deck between the
       <a href="/wiki/Flight_engineer" title="Flight engineer">
        flight engineer
       </a>
       's console and the bulkhead. On some aircraft that conducted a retiring supersonic flight, the flight engineers placed their caps in this expanded gap, wedging the cap when it shrank again.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To keep the cabin cool, Concorde used the fuel as a
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">
        heat sink
       </a>
       for the heat from the air conditioning.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The same method also cooled the hydraulics. During supersonic flight the surfaces forward from the cockpit became heated, and a visor was used to deflect much of this heat from directly reaching the cockpit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200114_116-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200114-116">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Concorde had
       <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_livery" title="Aircraft livery">
        livery
       </a>
       restrictions; the majority of the surface had to be covered with a
       <a href="/wiki/Anti-flash_white" title="Anti-flash white">
        highly reflective white
       </a>
       paint to avoid overheating the aluminium structure due to heating effects from supersonic flight at Mach 2. The white finish reduced the skin temperature by 6 to 11 degrees Celsius.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1996, Air France briefly painted F-BTSD in a predominantly blue livery, with the exception of the wings, in a promotional deal with
       <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">
        Pepsi
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this paint scheme, Air France was advised to remain at Mach 2 for no more than 20 minutes at a time, but there was no restriction at speeds under Mach 1.7. F-BTSD was used because it was not scheduled for any long flights that required extended Mach 2 operations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
        <a href="#cite_note-119">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_issues">
        Structural issues
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Structural issues">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_fuel_trim.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="779" data-file-width="439" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Concorde_fuel_trim.svg/170px-Concorde_fuel_trim.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Concorde_fuel_trim.svg/255px-Concorde_fuel_trim.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Concorde_fuel_trim.svg/340px-Concorde_fuel_trim.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_fuel_trim.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Fuel pitch trim
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Due to its high speeds, large forces were applied to the aircraft during banks and turns, and caused twisting and distortion of the aircraft's structure. In addition there were concerns over maintaining precise control at supersonic speeds. Both of these issues were resolved by active ratio changes between the inboard and outboard
       <a href="/wiki/Elevon" title="Elevon">
        elevons
       </a>
       , varying at differing speeds including supersonic. Only the innermost elevons, which are attached to the stiffest area of the wings, were active at high speed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200178_120-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200178-120">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, the narrow fuselage meant that the aircraft flexed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nova_72-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-nova-72">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was visible from the rear passengers' viewpoints.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popular_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-popular-121">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When any aircraft passes the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_mach" title="Critical mach">
        critical mach
       </a>
       of that particular airframe, the
       <a href="/wiki/Center_of_pressure_(fluid_mechanics)" title="Center of pressure (fluid mechanics)">
        centre of pressure
       </a>
       shifts rearwards. This causes a pitch down moment on the aircraft if the centre of gravity remains where it was. The engineers designed the wings in a specific manner to reduce this shift, but there was still a shift of about 2 metres. This could have been countered by the use of
       <a href="/wiki/Trim_tab" title="Trim tab">
        trim controls
       </a>
       , but at such high speeds this would have dramatically increased drag. Instead, the distribution of fuel along the aircraft was shifted during acceleration and deceleration to move the centre of gravity, effectively acting as an auxiliary trim control.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fueltrim_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fueltrim-122">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Range">
        Range
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Range">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       To fly non-stop across the Atlantic Ocean, Concorde required the greatest supersonic
       <a href="/wiki/Range_(aeronautics)" title="Range (aeronautics)">
        range
       </a>
       of any aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
        <a href="#cite_note-123">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was achieved by a combination of engines which were highly efficient at supersonic speeds,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [N 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nova_72-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-nova-72">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a slender fuselage with high
       <a href="/wiki/Fineness_ratio" title="Fineness ratio">
        fineness ratio
       </a>
       , and a complex wing shape for a high
       <a href="/wiki/Lift-to-drag_ratio" title="Lift-to-drag ratio">
        lift-to-drag ratio
       </a>
       . This also required carrying only a modest payload and a high fuel capacity, and the aircraft was trimmed with precision to avoid unnecessary drag.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deltawing_17-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-deltawing-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fueltrim_122-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-fueltrim-122">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Nevertheless, soon after Concorde began flying, a Concorde "B" model was designed with slightly larger fuel capacity and slightly larger wings with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leading_edge_slats" title="Leading edge slats">
        leading edge slats
       </a>
       to improve aerodynamic performance at all speeds, with the objective of expanding the range to reach markets in new regions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
        <a href="#cite_note-125">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It featured more powerful engines with sound deadening and without the fuel-hungry and noisy
       <a href="/wiki/Afterburner" title="Afterburner">
        afterburner
       </a>
       . It was speculated that it was reasonably possible to create an engine with up to 25% gain in efficiency over the Rolls-Royce/Snecma Olympus 593.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This would have given 500 mi (805 km) additional range and a greater payload, making new commercial routes possible. This was cancelled due in part to poor sales of Concorde, but also to the rising cost of aviation fuel in the 1970s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_concerns">
        Radiation concerns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radiation concerns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg/220px-ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg/330px-ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg/440px-ConcordeFuselageSinsheim.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          External view of Concorde's
          <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">
           fuselage
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg/220px-Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg/330px-Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg/440px-Concorde_passenger_cabin.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">
           British Airways
          </a>
          Concorde interior. The narrow fuselage permitted only a 4-abreast seating with limited headroom.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Concorde's high cruising altitude meant passengers received almost twice the
       <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">
        flux
       </a>
       of extraterrestrial
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionising_radiation" title="Ionising radiation">
        ionising radiation
       </a>
       as those travelling on a conventional long-haul flight.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
        <a href="#cite_note-128">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concradi_129-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-concradi-129">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Upon Concorde's introduction, it was speculated that this exposure during supersonic travels would increase the likelihood of skin cancer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to the proportionally reduced flight time, the overall
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalent_dose" title="Equivalent dose">
        equivalent dose
       </a>
       would normally be less than a conventional flight over the same distance.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unusual
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">
        solar activity
       </a>
       might lead to an increase in incident radiation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To prevent incidents of excessive radiation exposure, the flight deck had a radiometer and an instrument to measure the rate of decrease of radiation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concradi_129-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-concradi-129">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If the radiation level became too high, Concorde would descend below 47,000 feet (14,000 m).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cabin_pressurisation">
        Cabin pressurisation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cabin pressurisation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_cabin" title="Aircraft cabin">
        Airliner cabins
       </a>
       were usually maintained at a pressure equivalent to 6,000–8,000 feet (1,800–2,400 m) elevation. Concorde's
       <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">
        pressurisation
       </a>
       was set to an altitude at the lower end of this range, 6,000 feet (1,800 m).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde's maximum cruising altitude was 60,000 feet (18,000 m); subsonic airliners typically cruise below 40,000 feet (12,000 m).
      </p>
      <p>
       A sudden
       <a href="/wiki/Uncontrolled_decompression" title="Uncontrolled decompression">
        reduction in cabin pressure
       </a>
       is hazardous to all passengers and crew.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
        <a href="#cite_note-134">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Above 50,000 feet (15,000 m), a sudden cabin depressurisation would leave a "
       <a href="/wiki/Time_of_useful_consciousness" title="Time of useful consciousness">
        time of useful consciousness
       </a>
       " up to 10–15 seconds for a conditioned athlete.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
        <a href="#cite_note-135">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At Concorde's altitude, the air density is very low; a breach of cabin integrity would result in a loss of pressure severe enough that the plastic emergency oxygen masks installed on other passenger jets would not be effective and passengers would soon suffer from
       <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">
        hypoxia
       </a>
       despite quickly donning them. Concorde was equipped with smaller windows to reduce the rate of loss in the event of a breach,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENunn1993341_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENunn1993341-136">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a reserve air supply system to augment cabin air pressure, and a rapid descent procedure to bring the aircraft to a safe altitude. The FAA enforces minimum emergency descent rates for aircraft and noting Concorde's higher operating altitude, concluded that the best response to pressure loss would be a rapid descent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
        <a href="#cite_note-137">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Continuous_positive_airway_pressure" title="Continuous positive airway pressure">
        Continuous positive airway pressure
       </a>
       would have delivered pressurised oxygen directly to the pilots through masks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENunn1993341_136-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENunn1993341-136">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_characteristics">
        Flight characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Flight characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg/220px-Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg/330px-Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg/440px-Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_at_Baginton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_156846.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concorde performing a low-level fly-by at an air show in August 1981
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       While subsonic commercial jets took eight hours to fly from New York to Paris, the average supersonic flight time on the transatlantic routes was just under 3.5 hours. Concorde had a maximum cruise altitude of 18,300 metres (60,039 ft) and an average cruise speed of Mach 2.02, about 1155
       <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">
        knots
       </a>
       (2140 km/h or 1334 mph), more than twice the speed of conventional aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchrader198964_138-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchrader198964-138">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       With no other civil traffic operating at its cruising altitude of about 56,000 ft (17,000 m), Concorde had exclusive use of dedicated oceanic airways, or "tracks", separate from the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Tracks" title="North Atlantic Tracks">
        North Atlantic Tracks
       </a>
       , the routes used by other aircraft to cross the Atlantic. Due to the significantly less variable nature of high altitude winds compared to those at standard cruising altitudes, these dedicated SST tracks had fixed co-ordinates, unlike the standard routes at lower altitudes, whose co-ordinates are replotted twice daily based on forecast weather patterns (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jetstream" title="Jetstream">
        jetstreams
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrlebar200484_139-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrlebar200484-139">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Concorde would also be cleared in a 15,000-foot (4,600 m) block, allowing for a slow climb from 45,000 to 60,000 ft (18,000 m) during the oceanic crossing as the fuel load gradually decreased.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In regular service, Concorde employed an efficient
       <i>
        cruise-climb
       </i>
       flight profile following take-off.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrlebar200492_141-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrlebar200492-141">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The delta-shaped wings required Concorde to adopt a higher
       <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">
        angle of attack
       </a>
       at low speeds than conventional aircraft, but it allowed the formation of large low pressure vortices over the entire upper wing surface, maintaining lift.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrlebar200444_142-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrlebar200444-142">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The normal landing speed was 170 miles per hour (274 km/h).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchrader198984_143-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchrader198984-143">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of this high angle, during a landing approach Concorde was on the "back side" of the
       <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_drag" title="Parasitic drag">
        drag force
       </a>
       curve, where raising the nose would increase the rate of descent; the aircraft was thus largely flown on the throttle and was fitted with an autothrottle to reduce the pilot's workload.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrlebar2004110_144-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrlebar2004110-144">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        The only thing that tells you that you're moving is that occasionally when you're flying over the subsonic aeroplanes you can see all these 747s 20,000 feet below you almost appearing to go backwards, I mean you are going 800 miles an hour or thereabouts faster than they are. The aeroplane was an absolute delight to fly, it handled beautifully. And remember we are talking about an aeroplane that was being designed in the late 1950s – mid 1960s. I think it's absolutely amazing and here we are, now in the 21st century, and it remains unique.
       </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        —
        <cite>
         John Hutchinson, Concorde Captain, "The World's Greatest Airliner" (2003)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
          <a href="#cite_note-145">
           [140]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Brakes_and_undercarriage">
        Brakes and undercarriage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Brakes and undercarriage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="3718" data-file-width="2830" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG/200px-Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG/300px-Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG/400px-Train_d%27atterrissage_Concorde_Musee_du_Bourget_P1020039.JPG 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Concorde main undercarriage
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_tail_gear.JPG">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Concorde_tail_gear.JPG/200px-Concorde_tail_gear.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Concorde_tail_gear.JPG/300px-Concorde_tail_gear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Concorde_tail_gear.JPG/400px-Concorde_tail_gear.JPG 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Tail bumper of Concorde G-BOAG at the
          <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Flight" title="Museum of Flight">
           Museum of Flight
          </a>
          in Seattle
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Because of the way Concorde's delta-wing generated lift, the undercarriage had to be unusually strong and tall to allow for the angle of attack at low speed. At
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotation_(aviation)" title="Rotation (aviation)">
        rotation
       </a>
       , Concorde would rise to a high angle of attack, about 18 degrees. Prior to rotation the wing generated almost no lift, unlike typical aircraft wings. Combined with the high airspeed at rotation (199 knots
       <a href="/wiki/Indicated_airspeed" title="Indicated airspeed">
        indicated airspeed
       </a>
       ), this increased the stresses on the main undercarriage in a way that was initially unexpected during the development and required a major redesign.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to the high angle needed at rotation, a small set of wheels were added aft to prevent
       <a href="/wiki/Tailstrike" title="Tailstrike">
        tailstrikes
       </a>
       . The main undercarriage units swing towards each other to be stowed but due to their great height also need to contract in length telescopically before swinging to clear each other when stowed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The four main wheel tyres on each
       <a href="/wiki/Bogie" title="Bogie">
        bogie
       </a>
       unit are inflated to 232 lb/sq in (1,600 kPa). The twin-wheel nose undercarriage retracts forwards and its tyres are inflated to a pressure of 191 lb/sq in (1,320 kPa), and the wheel assembly carries a spray deflector to prevent standing water being thrown up into the engine intakes. The tyres are rated to a maximum speed on the runway of 250 mph (400 km/h).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The starboard nose wheel carries a single disc brake to halt wheel rotation during retraction of the undercarriage. The port nose wheel carries speed generators for the anti-skid braking system which prevents brake activation until nose and main wheels rotate at the same rate.
      </p>
      <p>
       Additionally, due to the high average take-off speed of 250 miles per hour (400 km/h), Concorde needed upgraded brakes. Like most airliners, Concorde has
       <a href="/wiki/Anti-lock_braking_system" title="Anti-lock braking system">
        anti-skid braking
       </a>
       – a system which prevents the tyres from losing traction when the brakes are applied for greater control during roll-out. The brakes, developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Dunlop_Rubber" title="Dunlop Rubber">
        Dunlop
       </a>
       , were the first carbon-based brakes used on an airliner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The use of carbon over equivalent steel brakes provided a weight-saving of 1,200 lb (540 kg).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen2001118_150-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen2001118-150">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Each wheel has multiple discs which are cooled by electric fans. Wheel sensors include brake overload, brake temperature, and tyre deflation. After a typical landing at Heathrow, brake temperatures were around 300–400 °C (570–750 °F). Landing Concorde required a minimum of 6,000 feet (1,800 m) runway length, this in fact being considerably less than the shortest runway Concorde ever actually landed on, that of
       <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Airport" title="Cardiff Airport">
        Cardiff Airport
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Droop_nose">
        Droop nose
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Droop nose">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Droop-nose" title="Droop-nose">
        Droop-nose
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Concorde's drooping nose, developed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshall_Aerospace" title="Marshall Aerospace">
        Marshall's of Cambridge
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Airport" title="Cambridge Airport">
        Cambridge Airport
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concorde_nose_152-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Concorde_nose-152">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       enabled the aircraft to switch between being streamlined to reduce drag and achieve optimal aerodynamic efficiency without obstructing the pilot's view during taxi, take-off, and landing operations. Due to the high angle of attack, the long pointed nose obstructed the view and necessitated the capability to droop. The droop nose was accompanied by a moving visor that retracted into the nose prior to being lowered. When the nose was raised to horizontal, the visor would rise in front of the cockpit windscreen for aerodynamic streamlining.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concorde_nose_152-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Concorde_nose-152">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="1023" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg/220px-Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg/330px-Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg/440px-Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concorde_landing_Farnborough_Fitzgerald.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concorde landing at
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farnborough_Aerodrome" title="Farnborough Aerodrome">
          Farnborough
         </a>
         in September 1974
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A controller in the cockpit allowed the visor to be retracted and the nose to be lowered to 5° below the standard horizontal position for taxiing and take-off. Following take-off and after clearing the airport, the nose and visor were raised. Prior to landing, the visor was again retracted and the nose lowered to 12.5° below horizontal for maximal visibility. Upon landing the nose was raised to the 5° position to avoid the possibility of damage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concorde_nose_152-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Concorde_nose-152">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The US
       <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">
        Federal Aviation Administration
       </a>
       had objected to the restrictive visibility of the visor used on the first two prototype Concordes, which had been designed before a suitable high-temperature window glass had become available, and thus requiring alteration before the FAA would permit Concorde to serve US airports. This led to the redesigned visor used on the production and the four pre-production aircraft (101, 102, 201, and 202).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOwen200184_153-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOwen200184-153">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The nose window and visor glass, needed to endure temperatures in excess of 100 °C (210 °F) at supersonic flight, were developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Pilkington" title="Pilkington">
        Triplex
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:left;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_history">
        Operational history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Operational history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Concorde_aircraft_histories" title="Concorde aircraft histories">
        Concorde aircraft histories
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1973_Solar_Eclipse_Mission">
        1973 Solar Eclipse Mission
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concorde&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 1973 Solar Eclipse Mission">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        Concorde 001
       </i>
       was modified with rooftop portholes for use on the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_June_30,_1973" title="Solar eclipse of June 30, 1973">
        1973
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Eclipse" title="Solar Eclipse">
        Solar Eclipse
       </a>
       mission and equipped with observation instruments. It performed the longest observation of a solar eclipse to date, about 74 minutes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vice-Motherboard-2016-03-09_155-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vice-Motherboard-2016-03-09-155">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Air_France_Concorde_Jonsson.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Air France Concorde at
         <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle_Airport" title="Charles de Gaulle Airport">
          CDG Airport
         </a>
         in 2003
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Scheduled flights began on 21 January 1976 on the London–
       <a href="/wiki/Bahrain_International_Airport" title="Bahrain International Airport">
        Bahrain
       </a>
       and Paris–
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro%E2%80%93Gale%C3%A3o_International_Airport" title="Rio de Janeiro–Galeão International Airport">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       (via
       <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">
        Dakar
       </a>
       ) routes,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with BA flights using the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Speedbird" title="Speedbird">
         Speedbird
        </a>
        Concorde
       </i>
       call sign to notify air traffic control of the aircraft's unique abilities and restrictions, but the French using their normal call signs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Paris-
       <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">
        Caracas
       </a>
       route (via
       <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">
        Azores
       </a>
       ) began on 10 April. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States">
        US Congress
       </a>
       had just banned Concorde landings in the US, mainly due to citizen protest over
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonic_booms" title="Sonic booms">
        sonic booms
       </a>
       , preventing launch on the coveted North Atlantic routes. The US Secretary of Transportation,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Thaddeus_Coleman,_Jr." title="William Thaddeus Coleman, Jr.">
        William Coleman
       </a>
       , gave permission for Concorde service to
       <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">
        Washington Dulles International Airport
       </a>
       , and Air France and British Airways simultaneously began a thrice-weekly service to Dulles on 24 May 1976.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to low demand, Air France cancelled its Washington service in October 1982, while British Airways cancelled it in November 1994.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When the US ban on JFK Concorde operations was lifted in February 1977, New York banned Concorde locally. The ban came to an end on 17 October 1977 when the
       <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">
        Supreme Court of the United States
       </a>
       declined to overturn a lower court's ruling rejecting efforts by the
       <a href="/wiki/Port_Authority_of_New_York_and_New_Jersey" title="Port Authority of New York and New Jersey">
        Port Authority
       </a>
       and a grass-roots campaign led by
       <a href="/wiki/Carol_Berman" title="Carol Berman">
        Carol Berman
       </a>
       to continue the ban.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In spite of complaints about noise, the noise report noted that
       <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">
        Air Force One
       </a>
       , at the time a
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing_C-137_Stratoliner" title="Boeing C-137 Stratoliner">
        Boeing VC-137
       </a>
